Apple's decision to use the Lightning connector for its battery cable appears to be a strategic move to generate revenue from other companies through the production of adapters. This decision limits the compatibility of the cable with various external battery brands, potentially extending the lifespan of the cable. However, it is unlikely that Apple has implemented software restrictions to prevent the use of external batteries, as they have a history of engaging in such practices. Nevertheless, we should discourage such practices as they result in companies profiting from the production of adapters for the cable battery. Apple's reluctance to switch from the Lightning connector for an extended period can be attributed to this strategy. Alternatively, Anakef and other manufacturers may consider developing a battery with the same cable attachment as Apple, potentially capitalizing on this market opportunity. This would be a mutually beneficial arrangement for both parties, as Apple would continue to generate revenue from the sale of adapters, while manufacturers would be able to produce batteries that are compatible with Apple devices.

Something I have learned is that apple takes number 1 place for the least repairable stuff. Trying to open up a dead apple watch to remove the lithium battery for recycling was hell. The screws are really easy to find, but you need apples own screwdrivers because of their special screws. We did not have that. So the easiest way was through the screen. Smashing it up and picking out the lithium battery was a success! The same goes for the iphones, similar screws but also different. Smash that screen!  I would say the closest contestant I have met with notorious teardown would be JBL speakers. Yes they use universal screws, but the placements really makes you ask yourself "How the heck did they apply them?!". Plus they like to hide them

This is an interesting teardown. From the outside it makes sense to have this laminated glass, just to reduce the overall weight, but inside lenses being the same material, which is highly prone to scratches - that's a dangerous game to play and I'm not sure the most practical of options. I won't deny the fact, that this does look like a feat of engineering from one side and from the other you have dumb solution like a battery pack, that sits in your pocket. Another thing, because nobody talks about it, I wonder how hot this thing gets and how loud the fans are operating? As a basic premise - okay, but as a previous VR user - this still feels like a chore to use. My thinking is, that VR will take off the moment technology will allow it to be used as simply and effortlessly as putting the glasses on, so we have possibly about a decade for that? Especially with batteries: batteries with sodium won't do much, as this seems to be another step, but nano batteries with tungsten and graphite materials offering possibly much more efficient energy storage. So to me as of now all those VR headsets are an expensive gimmick, that are fun to try, but nowhere near being long time use devices, for the simple fact of lack of software support and overall comfort.

Here are some key takeaways from the video:  1. The Apple Vision Pro headset provides an extremely immersive virtual reality experience that impressed even an Android/PC user like the video creator. The hardware and software work together seamlessly to create very realistic virtual environments.  2. However, the exterior "glass" surface is actually plastic laminate that scratches easily at a Mohs hardness level of 3-4. Scratches could interfere with the headset's cameras and sensors. Replacement costs are very high.  3. The aluminum body is very sturdy, with no flex when bent. But there are no visible screws, making repairs very difficult. Many screw types and ribbons cables inside make repairability low.  4. Dual micro-OLED screens with 23 million pixels each provide sharp, vivid visuals. An M2 chip and custom R1 chip work together for low 12ms latency between movement and visuals.  5. Accessibility could be improved for those with impairments, like alternate click options beyond finger taps. Battery life is a bit short at 2 hours - anchor accessories can help extend it.  In summary, the Vision Pro pushes VR technology forward significantly, but sacrifices repairability and durability for the sake of sleek, compact design. The high price also limits wider adoption.

More accessibility notes: Apple Vision Pro is not usable for people with a lot of eye conditionsâ€” unlike other headsets. Besides the fact that corrective lens inserts are not available for people with diplopia, the choice to go with eye tracking for cursor selection causes unique problems for the Apple Vision Pro.  If you have amblyopia, (at least) one of your eyes doesn't have a full range of motion. If you have nystagmus, your eyes involuntarily move sometimes. If you have problems with central vision (e.g., macular degeneration, cone dystrophy, diabetic retinopathy), you may have to rely primarily or entirely on your peripheral visionâ€” so where your pupil is pointing is never what you're actually looking at.  These are all likely to present serious problems for the kind of input Vision Pro is designed for, and I would assume that at this stage any of them could be showstoppers. Maybe Apple can compensate for some of them in software, but support for alternative pointing methods would be a great, flexible tool for visually impaired people of many kinds!

I'm glad Apple invented this 10:03 :  Predecessors Tabula scalata Main article: Tabula scalata Corrugated images that change when viewed from different angles predate the development of lenticular printing. A few examples from the paleolithic era exist in French caves.[2][3] Tabula scalata or "turning pictures" were popular in England since the 16th century.[4] Extant double paintings, with two distinct images on a corrugated panel, are known from the 17th century.[5][6]  H.C.J. Deeks used a similar technique with minute vertical corrugations pressed into photographic paper and then exposed to two different images from two different angles.[7] Under a 1906 patent H.C.J. Deeks & Co marketed a Puzzle Post Card or Photochange Post Card. In 1907 a Colorchange Post Card followed, featuring identical pictures on each side of the corrugations that were sprayed with different "liquid pigment or coloring matter" on (parts of) each side.[8]  Barrier grid autostereograms and animation Main article: Barrier grid animation and stereography  Berthier's diagram: A-B=glass plate, with a-b=opaque lines, P=Picture, O=Eyes, c-n=blocked and allowed views (Le Cosmos 05â€“1896) The oldest known publication about using a line sheet as a parallax barrier to produce an autostereogram is found in an article by Auguste Berthier in the French scientific magazine "Le Cosmos" of May 1896.[9] Berthier's idea was hardly noticed, but American inventor Frederic Eugene Ives had more success with his similar parallax stereogram since 1901. He also patented the technique for a "Changeable sign, picture, &c." in 1903, which showed different pictures from different angles (instead of one stereoscopic image from the right angle and distance). LÃ©on Gaumont introduced Ives' pictures in France and encouraged EugÃ¨ne Estanave to work on the technique. Estanave patented a barrier grid technique for animated autostereograms. Animated portrait photographs with line sheets were marketed for a while, mostly in the 1910s and 1920s. In the US "Magic Moving Picture" postcards with simple 3 phase animation or changing pictures were marketed after 1906. Maurice Bonnett improved barrier grid autostereography in the 1930s with his reliÃ¨phographie technique and scanning cameras.  On 11 April 1898 John Jacobson filed an application for US patent No. 624,043 (granted 2 May 1899) for a Stereograph of an interlaced stereoscopic picture and "a transparent mount for said picture having a corrugated or channeled surface".[10] The corrugated lines or channels were not yet really lenticular, but this is the first known autostereogram that used a corrugated transparent surface rather than the opaque lines of most barrier grid stereograms.
